<p><b>Genocide</b> is the intentional destruction of a people—usually defined as an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">national</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_humans)" title="Race (classification of humans)">racial</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> group—in whole or in part. <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Lemkin" title="Raphael Lemkin">Raphael Lemkin</a> coined the term in 1944,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schab_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-schab-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPower200322–29_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPower200322–29-2">[2]</a></sup> combining the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a href="/wiki/Genos" title="Genos">γένος</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">genos</i></span>, "race, people") with the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">-caedo</i></span> ("act of killing").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1948, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Genocide_Convention" title="Genocide Convention">Genocide Convention</a> defined genocide as any of five "acts committed with intent to destroy, in whole or in part, a national, ethnic, racial or religious group, as such." These five acts were: killing members of the group, causing them serious bodily or mental harm, imposing living conditions intended to destroy the group, preventing births, and forcibly transferring children out of the group. Victims are targeted because of their real or perceived membership of a group, not randomly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNLegalDef_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNLegalDef-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Political_Instability_Task_Force" title="Political Instability Task Force">Political Instability Task Force</a> estimated that 43 genocides occurred between 1956 and 2016, resulting in about 50 million deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNHCR" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a> estimated that a further 50 million had been displaced by such episodes of violence up to 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[8]</a></sup> Genocide, especially large-scale genocide, is widely considered to signify the epitome of human <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> As a label, it is contentious because it is moralizing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and has been used as a type of moral category since the late 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1612" data-file-width="2256" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg/220px-Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg/330px-Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg/440px-Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dup%C4%83_trecerea_unui_convoi_%C3%AEntre_Birzula_%C8%99i_Grozdovca.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/1941_Odessa_massacre" title="1941 Odessa massacre">1941 Odessa massacre</a>, in which Jewish deportees were killed outside Brizula (now <a href="/wiki/Podilsk" title="Podilsk">Podilsk</a>) during <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/220px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/330px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/440px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human skulls at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyamata_Genocide_Memorial" title="Nyamata Genocide Memorial">Nyamata Genocide Memorial</a> in Rwanda</div></div></div>
<p>Before the term <i>genocide</i> was coined, there were various ways of describing such events. Some languages already had words for such killings, including German (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/V%C3%B6lkermord" title="de:Völkermord">Völkermord</a></i></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'murder of a people') and Polish (<span title="Polish-language text"><i lang="pl"><a class="extiw" href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/ludob%C3%B3jstwo" title="pl:ludobójstwo">ludobójstwo</a></i></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'killing of a people or nation').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platen_1831_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platen_1831-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Böttcher-Berger-Krolop-Zimmermann_1985_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Böttcher-Berger-Krolop-Zimmermann_1985-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1941, when describing the "methodical, merciless butchery" of "scores of thousands" of Russians by Nazi troops during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_invasion_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="German invasion of the Soviet Union">German invasion of the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> spoke of "a crime without a name".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1944, <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Lemkin" title="Raphael Lemkin">Raphael Lemkin</a> coined the term <i>genocide</i> as a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_word" title="Hybrid word">hybrid combination</a> of the Ancient Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">γένος</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">génos</i></span>) 'race, people' with the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">caedere</i></span>, 'to kill';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemkin200879_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemkin200879-21">[21]</a></sup> his book <i>Axis Rule in Occupied Europe</i> (1944) describes the implementation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> policies in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupied_Europe" title="Occupied Europe">occupied Europe</a> and mentions earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_killings" title="Mass killings">mass killings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ushmm_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-ushmm-22">[22]</a></sup>
After reading about the 1921 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Talat_Pasha" title="Assassination of Talat Pasha">assassination of Talat Pasha</a>, the main architect of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a>, by Armenian <a href="/wiki/Soghomon_Tehlirian" title="Soghomon Tehlirian">Soghomon Tehlirian</a>, Lemkin asked his professor why there was no law under which Talat could be charged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schab_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-schab-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> He later explained that "as a lawyer, I thought that a crime should not be punished by the victims, but should be punished by a court."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lemkin defined genocide as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>New conceptions require new terms. By "genocide" we mean the destruction of a nation or of an ethnic group. This new word, coined by the author to denote an old practice in its modern development, is made from the ancient Greek word <i>genos</i> (race, tribe) and the Latin <i>cide</i> (killing), thus corresponding in its formation to such words as tyrannicide, homicide, infanticide, etc. Generally speaking, genocide does not necessarily mean the immediate destruction of a nation, except when accomplished by mass killings of all members of a nation. It is intended rather to signify a coordinated plan of different actions aiming at the destruction of essential foundations of the life of national groups, with the aim of annihilating the groups themselves. The objectives of such a plan would be disintegration of the political and social institutions, of culture, language, national feelings, religion, and the economic existence of national groups, and the destruction of the personal security, liberty, health, dignity, and even the lives of the individuals belonging to such groups. Genocide is directed against the national group as an entity, and the actions involved are directed against individuals, not in their individual capacity, but as members of the national group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELemkin200879_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELemkin200879-21">[21]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The preamble to the 1948 <a href="/wiki/Genocide_Convention" title="Genocide Convention">Genocide Convention</a> (CPPCG) notes that instances of genocide have taken place throughout history;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPPCG_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPPCG-26">[26]</a></sup> it was not until Lemkin coined the term and the prosecution of perpetrators of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a> that the United Nations defined the crime of genocide under <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> in the Genocide Convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ushmm_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-ushmm-22">[22]</a></sup> It was several years before the term was widely adopted by the international community. When the Nuremberg trials revealed the inadequacy of phrases like "Germanization", "crimes against humanity" and "mass murder", scholars of international law reached agreement that Lemkin's work provided a conceptual framework for Nazi crimes. A 1946 headline in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> announced that "Genocide Is the New Name for the Crime Fastened on the Nazi Leaders";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> the word was used in indictments at the Nuremberg trials, held from 1945, but solely as a descriptive term, not yet as a formal legal term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolocaustEncyclopedia2014_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-HolocaustEncyclopedia2014-29">[29]</a></sup> The so-called Polish Genocide Trials of <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Greiser" title="Arthur Greiser">Arthur Greiser</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amon_Leopold_Goth" title="Amon Leopold Goth">Amon Leopold Goth</a> in 1946 were the first trials in which judgments included the term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">Crime</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-criminalization_view">Pre-criminalization view</span></h3>
<p>Before genocide was made a crime against national law, it was considered a sovereign right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erickson_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erickson-31">[31]</a></sup> When Lemkin asked about a way to punish the perpetrators of the Armenian genocide, a law professor told him: "Consider the case of a farmer who owns a flock of chickens. He kills them and this is his business. If you interfere, you are trespassing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> As late as 1959, many world leaders still "believed states had a right to commit genocide against people within their borders", according to political scientist <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Irvin-Erickson" title="Douglas Irvin-Erickson">Douglas Irvin-Erickson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erickson_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erickson-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_law">International law</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg/250px-Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg/375px-Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg/500px-Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auschwitz_Resistance_280_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sonderkommando" title="Sonderkommando">Sonderkommando</a></i> burn corpses of Jews in pits at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a> II-Birkenau, an <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camp</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>After the Holocaust, which had been perpetrated by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> prior to and during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Lemkin successfully campaigned for the universal acceptance of international laws defining and forbidding genocides. In 1946, the first session of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> adopted a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_96" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 96">resolution</a> that affirmed genocide was a crime under international law and enumerated examples of such events (but did not provide a full legal definition of the crime). In 1948, the UN General Assembly adopted the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Prevention_and_Punishment_of_the_Crime_of_Genocide" title="Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide">Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide</a></i> (CPPCG) which defined the crime of genocide for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Genocide is a denial of the right of existence of entire human groups, as homicide is the denial of the right to live of individual human beings; such denial of the right of existence shocks the conscience of mankind, results in great losses to humanity in the form of cultural and other contributions represented by these human groups, and is contrary to moral law and the spirit and aims of the United Nations. Many instances of such crimes of genocide have occurred when racial, religious, political and other groups have been destroyed, entirely or in part.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>UN Resolution 96(1), 11 December 1946</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The <i>CPPCG</i> was adopted by the UN General Assembly on 9 December 1948<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNLegalDef_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNLegalDef-5">[5]</a></sup> and came into effect on 12 January 1951 (Resolution 260 (III)). It contains an internationally recognized definition of genocide which has been incorporated into the national criminal legislation of many countries and was also adopted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court" title="Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court">Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court</a>, which established the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> (ICC). Article II of the Convention defines genocide as:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... any of the following acts committed with <a href='#"Intent_to_destroy"'>intent to destroy</a>, <a href='#"In_part"'>in whole or in part</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">national</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic" title="Ethnic">ethnical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial" title="Racial">racial</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious" title="Religious">religious</a> group, as such:</p><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li>(a) Killing members of the group;</li>
<li>(b) Causing serious <a href='#"bodily_harm"'>bodily</a> or mental harm to members of the group;</li>
<li>(c) Deliberately inflicting on the group conditions of life calculated to bring about its physical destruction in whole or in part;</li>
<li>(d) Imposing measures intended to prevent births within the group;</li>
<li>(e) Forcibly transferring children of the group to another group.</li></ul>
</div></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Incitement_to_genocide" title="Incitement to genocide">Incitement to genocide</a> is recognized as a separate crime under international law and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inchoate_crime" title="Inchoate crime">inchoate crime</a> which does not require genocide to have taken place to be prosecutable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ushmm2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ushmm2-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first draft of the convention included political killings; these provisions were removed in a political and diplomatic compromise following objections from many diverse countries, and originally promoted by the <a href="/wiki/World_Jewish_Congress" title="World Jewish Congress">World Jewish Congress</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Lemkin" title="Raphael Lemkin">Raphael Lemkin</a>'s conception, with some scholars popularly emphasizing in literature the role of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> a permanent <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> member. The Soviets argued that the convention's definition should follow the etymology of the term,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staub_1992,_p._8_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staub_1992,_p._8-37">[37]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> in particular may have feared greater international scrutiny of the country's political killings, such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Purge" title="Great Purge">Great Purge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Lemkin, who coined <i>genocide</i>, approached the Soviet delegation as the resolution vote came close to reassure the Soviets that there was no conspiracy against them; none in the Soviet-led bloc opposed the resolution, which passed unanimously in December 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss-Wendt_2005_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-Wendt_2005-39">[39]</a></sup> Other nations, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> feared that including political groups in the definition would invite international intervention in domestic politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staub_1992,_p._8_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Staub_1992,_p._8-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1951, Lemkin was saying that the Soviet Union was the only state that could be indicted for genocide, his concept of genocide, as outlined in <i>Axis Rule in Occupied Europe</i>, covering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalinist" title="Stalinist">Stalinist</a> deportations as genocide by default, and differing in many ways from the adopted Genocide Convention. From a 21st-century perspective, it was such a broad coverage that it would include any grossly <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> violation as genocide, and that many events deemed by Lemkin genocidal did not amount to genocide. As the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> began, this change was the result of Lemkin's turn to <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a> in an attempt to convince the United States to ratify the Genocide Convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiss-Wendt_2005_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-Wendt_2005-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intent">Intent</span></h3>
<p>Under international law, genocide has two mental (<i><a href="/wiki/Mens_rea" title="Mens rea">mens rea</a></i>) elements: the general mental element and the element of specific intent (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolus_specialis" title="Dolus specialis">dolus specialis</a></i>). The general element refers to whether the prohibited acts were committed with intent, knowledge, recklessness, or negligence. For most serious international crimes, including genocide, the requirement is that the perpetrator act with intent. The Rome Statute defines intent as meaning to engage in the conduct and, in relation to consequences, as meaning to cause that consequence or being "aware that it will occur in the ordinary course of events".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The specific intent element defines the purpose of committing the acts: "to destroy in whole or in part, a national, ethnical, racial or religious group, as such". The specific intent is a core factor distinguishing genocide from other international crimes, such as war crimes or crimes against humanity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Intent_to_destroy.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Intent_to_destroy"'>"Intent to destroy"</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genocidal_intent" title="Genocidal intent">Genocidal intent</a></div>
<p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> (ECHR) noted in its judgement on <i>Jorgic v. Germany</i> case that, in 1992, the majority of legal scholars took the narrow view that "intent to destroy" in the CPPCG meant the intended physical-biological destruction of the protected group, and that this was still the majority opinion. But the ECHR also noted that a minority took a broader view, and did not consider biological-physical destruction to be necessary, as the intent to destroy a national, racial, religious or ethnic group was enough to qualify as genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the same judgement, the ECHR reviewed the judgements of several international and municipal courts. It noted that the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_former_Yugoslavia" title="International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia">International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> had agreed with the narrow interpretation (that biological-physical destruction was necessary for an act to qualify as genocide). The ECHR also noted that at the time of its judgement, apart from courts in Germany (which had taken a broad view), that there had been few cases of genocide under other Convention states' <a href="/wiki/Municipal_law" title="Municipal law">municipal laws</a>, and that "There are no reported cases in which the courts of these States have defined the type of group destruction the perpetrator must have intended in order to be found guilty of genocide."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case of "Onesphore Rwabukombe", the German Supreme Court adhered to its previous judgement, and did not follow the narrow interpretation of the ICTY and the ICJ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22In_whole_or_in_part.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"In_whole_or_in_part"'>"In whole or in part"</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2731" data-file-width="3999" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg/220px-Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg/330px-Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg/440px-Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ambassador_Morgenthau%27s_Story_p314.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a> victims</div></div></div>
<p>The phrase "in whole or in part" has been subject to much discussion by scholars of international humanitarian law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_is_Genocide_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_Genocide-45">[45]</a></sup> In the Ruhashyankiko report of the United Nations it was once argued that the killing of only a single individual could be genocide if the intent to destroy the wider group was found in the murder,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> yet official court rulings have since contradicted this. The International Criminal Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia found in <i>Prosecutor v. Radislav Krstic – Trial Chamber I – Judgment – IT-98-33 (2001) ICTY8 (2 August 2001)</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> that Genocide had been committed. In <i>Prosecutor v. Radislav Krstic – Appeals Chamber – Judgment – IT-98-33 (2004) ICTY 7 (19 April 2004)</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PvRKappel_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-PvRKappel-48">[48]</a></sup> paragraphs 8, 9, 10, and 11 addressed the issue of <i>in part</i> and found that "the part must be a substantial part of that group. The aim of the Genocide Convention is to prevent the intentional destruction of entire human groups, and the part targeted must be significant enough to have an impact on the group as a whole." The Appeals Chamber goes into details of other cases and the opinions of respected commentators on the Genocide Convention to explain how they came to this conclusion.
</p><p>The judges continue in paragraph 12, "The determination of when the targeted part is substantial enough to meet this requirement may involve a number of considerations. The numeric size of the targeted part of the group is the necessary and important starting point, though not in all cases the ending point of the inquiry. The number of individuals targeted should be evaluated not only in absolute terms but also in relation to the overall size of the entire group. In addition to the numeric size of the targeted portion, its prominence within the group can be a useful consideration. If a specific part of the group is emblematic of the overall group or is essential to its survival, that may support a finding that the part qualifies as substantial within the meaning of Article 4 [of the Tribunal's Statute]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In paragraph 13 the judges raise the issue of the perpetrators' access to the victims: "The historical examples of genocide also suggest that the area of the perpetrators' activity and control, as well as the possible extent of their reach, should be considered. ... The intent to destroy formed by a perpetrator of genocide will always be limited by the opportunity presented to him. While this factor alone will not indicate whether the targeted group is substantial, it can—in combination with other factors—inform the analysis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PvRKappel_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-PvRKappel-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22A_national.2C_ethnic.2C_racial_or_religious_group.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"A_national,_ethnic,_racial_or_religious_group"'>"A national, ethnic, racial or religious group"</span></h4>
<p>The drafters of the CPPCG chose not to include political or social groups among the protected groups. Instead, they opted to focus on "stable" identities, attributes that are historically understood as being born into and unable or unlikely to change over time. This definition conflicts with modern conceptions of race as a social construct rather than innate fact and the practice of changing religion, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>International criminal courts have typically applied a mix of objective and subjective markers for determining whether or not a targeted population is a distinct group. Differences in language, physical appearance, religion, and cultural practices are objective criteria that may show that the groups are distinct. However, in circumstances such as the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsis</a> were often physically indistinguishable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In such a situation where a definitive answer based on objective markers is not clear, courts have turned to the subjective standard that "if a victim was perceived by a perpetrator as belonging to a protected group, the victim could be considered by the Chamber as a member of the protected group".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Stigmatization of the group by the perpetrators through legal measures, such as withholding citizenship, requiring the group to be identified, or isolating them from the whole could show that the perpetrators viewed the victims as a protected group.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span></h3>
<p>The Genocide Convention establishes five prohibited acts that, when committed with the requisite intent, amount to genocide. Although massacre-style killings are the most commonly identified and punished as genocide, the range of violence that is contemplated by the law is significantly broader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Killing_members_of_the_group">Killing members of the group</span></h4>
<p>While mass killing is not necessary for genocide to have been committed, it has been present in almost <a href="/wiki/List_of_genocides_by_death_toll" title="List of genocides by death toll">all recognized genocides</a>. A near-uniform pattern has emerged throughout history in which men and adolescent boys are singled out for murder in the early stages,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="UNHCR etc support that this happened in these cases, not that this is near-uniform pattern (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> such as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genocide_of_Yazidis_by_ISIL" title="Genocide of Yazidis by ISIL">genocide of the Yazidis by Daesh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Ottoman Turks' attack on the Armenians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_genocide" title="Rohingya genocide">Burmese security forces' attacks on the Rohingya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Men and boys are typically subject to "fast" killings, such as by gunshot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Women and girls are more likely to die slower deaths by slashing, burning, or as a result of sexual violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The jurisprudence of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda" title="International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda</a> (ICTR), among others, shows that both the initial executions and those that quickly follow other acts of extreme violence, such as <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>, are recognized as falling under the first prohibited act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>A less settled discussion is whether deaths that are further removed from the initial acts of violence can be addressed under this provision of the Genocide Convention. Legal scholars have posited, for example, that deaths resulting from other genocidal acts including causing serious bodily or mental harm or the successful deliberate infliction of conditions of life calculated to bring about physical destruction should be considered genocidal killings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Causing_serious_bodily_or_mental_harm_to_members_of_the_group_Article_II.28b.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Causing_serious_bodily_or_mental_harm_to_members_of_the_group_Article_II(b)">Causing serious bodily or mental harm to members of the group <i>Article II(b)</i></span></h4>
<p>This second prohibited act can encompass a wide range of non-fatal genocidal acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The ICTR and <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_former_Yugoslavia" title="International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia">International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia</a> (ICTY) have held that rape and sexual violence may constitute the second prohibited act of genocide by causing both physical and mental harm. In its landmark <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Akayesu" title="Jean-Paul Akayesu">Akayesu</a> decision, the ICTR held that rapes and sexual violence resulted in "physical and psychological destruction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Sexual violence is a hallmark of genocidal violence, with most genocidal campaigns explicitly or implicitly sanctioning it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup> It is estimated that 250,000 to 500,000 women were raped in the three months of the Rwandan genocide, many of whom were subjected to multiple rapes or <a href="/wiki/Gang_rape" title="Gang rape">gang rape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In Darfur, a systemic campaign of rape and often sexual mutilation was carried out<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and in Burma public mass rapes and gang rapes were inflicted on the Rohingya by Burmese security forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sexual_slavery" title="Sexual slavery">Sexual slavery</a> was documented in the Armenian genocide by the Ottoman Turks and Daesh's genocide of the Yazidi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Torture and other cruel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inhuman,_or_degrading_treatment" title="Inhuman, or degrading treatment">inhuman, or degrading treatment</a> or punishment, when committed with the requisite intent, are also genocide by causing serious bodily or mental harm to members of the group. The ICTY found that both experiencing a failed execution and watching the murder of one's family members may constitute torture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Independent_International_Commission_of_Inquiry_on_the_Syrian_Arab_Republic" title="Independent International Commission of Inquiry on the Syrian Arab Republic">Syrian Commission of Inquiry (COI)</a> also found that enslavement, removal of one's children into indoctrination or sexual slavery, and acts of physical and sexual violence rise to the level of torture, as well. While it was subject to some debate, the ICTY and, later, the Syrian COI held that under some circumstances deportation and forcible transfer may also cause serious bodily or mental harm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deliberately_inflicting_on_the_group_conditions_of_life_calculated_to_bring_about_its_physical_destruction">Deliberately inflicting on the group conditions of life calculated to bring about its physical destruction</span></h4>
<p>The third prohibited act is distinguished from the genocidal act of killing because the deaths are not immediate (or may not even come to pass), but rather create circumstances that do not support prolonged life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup> Due to the longer period of time before the actual destruction would be achieved, the ICTR held that courts must consider the duration of time the conditions are imposed as an element of the act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-69">[69]</a></sup> The drafters incorporated the act to account for the horrors of the Nazi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camps" title="Concentration camps">concentration camps</a> and to ensure that similar conditions never be imposed again. However, it could also apply to the Armenian death marches, the siege of <a href="/wiki/Sinjar_Mountains" title="Sinjar Mountains">Mount Sinjar</a> by Daesh, the deprivation of water and forcible deportation against ethnic groups in <a href="/wiki/Darfur_genocide" title="Darfur genocide">Darfur</a>, and the destruction and razing of communities in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>.
</p><p>The ICTR provided guidance into what constitutes a violation of the third act. In Akayesu, it identified "subjecting a group of people to a subsistence diet, systematic expulsion from homes and the reduction of essential medical services below minimum requirement"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> as rising to genocide. In Kayishema and Ruzindana, it extended the list to include: "lack of proper housing, clothing, hygiene and medical care or excessive work or physical exertion" among the conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-69">[69]</a></sup> It further noted that, in addition to deprivation of necessary resources, rape could also fit within this prohibited act.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imposing_measures_intended_to_prevent_births_within_the_group">Imposing measures intended to prevent births within the group</span></h4>
<p>The fourth prohibited act is aimed at preventing the protected group from regenerating through <a href="/wiki/Human_reproduction" title="Human reproduction">reproduction</a>. It encompasses acts with the single intent of affecting reproduction and intimate relationships, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Involuntary_sterilization" title="Involuntary sterilization">involuntary sterilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">forced abortion</a>, the prohibition of marriage, and long-term separation of men and women intended to prevent procreation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup> Rape has been found to violate the fourth prohibited act on two bases: where the rape was committed with the intent to impregnate a woman and thereby force her to carry a child of another group (in societies where group identity is determined by <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">patrilineal</a> identity) and where the person raped subsequently refuses to procreate as a result of the trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Accordingly, it can take into account both physical and mental measures imposed by the perpetrators.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forcibly_transferring_children_of_the_group_to_another_group">Forcibly transferring children of the group to another group</span></h4>
<p>The final prohibited act is the only prohibited act that does not lead to physical or biological destruction, but rather to destruction of the group as a cultural and social unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup> It occurs when children of the protected group are transferred to the perpetrator group. Boys are typically taken into the group by changing their names to those common of the perpetrator group, converting their religion, and using them for labor or as soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Girls who are transferred are not generally converted to the perpetrator group, but instead treated as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chattel_Slavery" title="Chattel Slavery">chattel</a>, as played out in both the Yazidi and Armenian genocides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Convention_on_the_Prevention_and_Punishment_of_the_Crime_of_Genocide">Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide</span></h3>
<p>The convention came into force as international law on 12 January 1951 after the minimum 20 countries became parties. At that time however, only two of the five permanent members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a> were parties to the treaty: France and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a>. The Soviet Union ratified in 1954, the United Kingdom in 1970, the People's Republic of China in 1983 (having replaced the Taiwan-based Republic of China on the UNSC in 1971), and the United States in 1988.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Schabas" title="William Schabas">William Schabas</a> has suggested that a permanent body as recommended by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitaker_Report_(United_Nations)" title="Whitaker Report (United Nations)">Whitaker Report</a> to monitor the implementation of the Genocide Convention, and require states to issue reports on their compliance with the convention (such as were incorporated into the United Nations <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optional_Protocol_to_the_Convention_against_Torture" title="Optional Protocol to the Convention against Torture">Optional Protocol to the Convention against Torture</a>), would make the convention more effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UN_Security_Council_Resolution_1674">UN Security Council Resolution 1674</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council_Resolution_1674" title="UN Security Council Resolution 1674">UN Security Council Resolution 1674</a>, adopted by the United Nations Security Council on 28 April 2006, "reaffirms the provisions of paragraphs 138 and 139 of the 2005 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Summit_Outcome_Document" title="World Summit Outcome Document">World Summit Outcome Document</a> regarding the responsibility to protect populations from genocide, war crimes, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> and crimes against humanity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution">resolution</a> committed the council to action to protect civilians in armed conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008 the UN Security Council adopted <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1820" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1820">resolution 1820</a>, which noted that "rape and other forms of sexual violence can constitute war crimes, crimes against humanity or a constitutive act with respect to genocide".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipal_law">Municipal law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genocide_under_municipal_laws" title="Genocide under municipal laws">Genocide under municipal laws</a></div>
<p>Since the Convention came into effect in January 1951 about 80 United Nations member states have passed legislation that incorporates the provisions of CPPCG into their <a href="/wiki/Municipal_law" title="Municipal law">municipal law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PVD_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-PVD-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_definitions_of_genocide">Other definitions of genocide</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genocide_definitions" title="Genocide definitions">Genocide definitions</a></div>
<p>Writing in 1998, Kurt Jonassohn and Karin Björnson stated that the CPPCG was a legal instrument resulting from a diplomatic compromise. As such the wording of the treaty is not intended to be a definition suitable as a research tool, and although it is used for this purpose, as it has international legal credibility that others lack, other <a href="/wiki/Genocide_definitions" title="Genocide definitions">genocide definitions</a> have also been proposed. Jonassohn and Björnson go on to say that none of these alternative definitions have gained widespread support for various reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonassohn-133-135_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonassohn-133-135-78">[78]</a></sup> Jonassohn and Björnson postulate that the major reason why no single generally accepted genocide definition has emerged is because academics have adjusted their focus to emphasise different periods and have found it expedient to use slightly different definitions. For example, Frank Chalk and Kurt Jonassohn studied all human history, while <a href="/wiki/Leo_Kuper" title="Leo Kuper">Leo Kuper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Rummel" title="Rudolph Rummel">Rudolph Rummel</a> in their more recent works concentrated on the 20th century, and <a href="/wiki/Helen_Fein" title="Helen Fein">Helen Fein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Harff" title="Barbara Harff">Barbara Harff</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ted_Gurr" title="Ted Gurr">Ted Gurr</a> have looked at post-World War II events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonassohn-133-135_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jonassohn-133-135-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_and_social_groups">Political and social groups</span></h3>
<p>The exclusion of social and political groups as targets of genocide in the CPPCG legal definition has been criticized by some historians and sociologists, for example, M. Hassan Kakar in his book <i>The Soviet Invasion and the Afghan Response, 1979–1982</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> argues that the international definition of genocide is too restricted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and that it should include political groups or any group so defined by the perpetrator and quotes Chalk and Jonassohn: "Genocide is a form of one-sided mass killing in which a state or other authority intends to destroy a group, as that group and membership in it are defined by the perpetrator."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In turn some states such as <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> include political groups as legitimate genocide victims in their anti-genocide laws.
</p><p>Harff and Gurr defined genocide as "the promotion and execution of policies by a state or its agents which result in the deaths of a substantial portion of a group ... [when] the victimized groups are defined primarily in terms of their communal characteristics, i.e., ethnicity, religion or nationality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Harff and Gurr also differentiate between genocides and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politicide" title="Politicide">politicides</a> by the characteristics by which members of a group are identified by the state. In genocides, the victimized groups are defined primarily in terms of their communal characteristics, i.e., ethnicity, religion or nationality. In politicides the victim groups are defined primarily in terms of their hierarchical position or political opposition to the regime and dominant groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Daniel D. Polsby and Don B. Kates, Jr. state that "we follow Harff's distinction between genocides and '<a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogroms</a>', which she describes as 'short-lived outbursts by mobs, which, although often condoned by authorities, rarely persist'. If the violence persists for long enough, however, Harff argues, the distinction between condonation and complicity collapses."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harff_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-harff-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Rummel, genocide has three different meanings. The ordinary meaning is murder by the government of people due to their national, ethnic, racial, or religious group membership. The legal meaning of genocide refers to the international treaty, the <i>Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide</i> (CPPCG). This also includes non-killings that in the end eliminate the group, such as preventing births or forcibly transferring children out of the group to another group. A generalized meaning of genocide is similar to the ordinary meaning but also includes government killings of political opponents or otherwise intentional murder. It is to avoid confusion regarding what meaning is intended that Rummel created the term <a href="/wiki/Democide" title="Democide">democide</a> for the third meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Highlighting the potential for state and non-state actors to commit genocide in the 21st century, for example, in failed states or as non-state actors acquiring weapons of mass destruction, Adrian Gallagher defined genocide as 'When a source of collective power (usually a state) intentionally uses its power base to implement a process of destruction in order to destroy a group (as defined by the perpetrator), in whole or in substantial part, dependent upon relative group size'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The definition upholds the centrality of intent, the multidimensional understanding of destroying, broadens the definition of group identity beyond that of the 1948 definition yet argues that a substantial part of a group has to be destroyed before it can be classified as genocide.
</p><p>Other definitions of genocide would include social groups defined by gender, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transgender_genocide">Transgender genocide</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transgender_genocide" title="Transgender genocide">Transgender genocide</a></div>
<p>With gender identity, transgender genocide emerged after widespread violence against <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender persons</a>, which has been systematically monitored since 2008 by the Trans Murder Monitoring project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In 2013, it was reported that, "Some international trans activists even started to introduce the term ‘transcide’ to reflect the continuously elevated level of deadly violence against trans people on a global scale and a coalition of NGOs from South America and Europe started the “Stop <a href="/wiki/Transgender_genocide" title="Transgender genocide">Trans Genocide</a>” campaign."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The term "trancide" follows an earlier term, <a href="/wiki/Gendercide" title="Gendercide">gendercide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Legal scholars have argued that the definition of genocide should be applied to transgender persons, or expanded to cover transgender persons, because they are victims of institutional discrimination, persecution, and violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-101">[101]</a></sup> Kritz argues that existing law does not apply and should be extended to protect transgender persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-101">[101]</a></sup> Similar arguments have been made regarding the legal definition of "<a href="/wiki/Crimes_against_humanity" title="Crimes against humanity">crimes against humanity</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Aside from legal studies, <a href="/wiki/Transgender_genocide" title="Transgender genocide">transgender genocide</a> has been examined by scholars of <a href="/wiki/Queer_studies" title="Queer studies">queer studies</a>, hate studies, and other fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_prosecution">International prosecution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_ad_hoc_tribunals">By <i>ad hoc</i> tribunals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2685" data-file-width="4491" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg/200px-Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg/300px-Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg/400px-Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuon_Chea_-_5_December_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nuon_Chea" title="Nuon Chea">Nuon Chea</a>, the Khmer Rouge's chief ideologist, before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambodian_Genocide_Tribunal" title="Cambodian Genocide Tribunal">Cambodian Genocide Tribunal</a> on 5 December 2011</div></div></div>
<p>All signatories to the CPPCG are required to prevent and punish acts of genocide, both in peace and wartime, though some barriers make this enforcement difficult. In particular, some of the signatories—namely, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">former Yugoslavia</a>—signed with the proviso that no claim of genocide could be brought against them at the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> without their consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Despite official protests from other signatories (notably <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>) on the ethics and legal standing of these reservations, the <a href="/wiki/Immunity_from_prosecution_(international_law)" title="Immunity from prosecution (international law)">immunity from prosecution</a> they grant has been invoked from time to time, as when the United States refused to allow a charge of genocide brought against it by former Yugoslavia following the 1999 <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is commonly accepted that, at least since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, genocide has been illegal under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Custom_(law)" title="Custom (law)">customary international law</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Peremptory_norm" title="Peremptory norm">peremptory norm</a>, as well as under <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">conventional international law</a>. Acts of genocide are generally difficult to establish for prosecution because a chain of accountability must be established. International criminal courts and tribunals function primarily because the states involved are incapable or unwilling to prosecute crimes of this magnitude themselves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Nuremberg_Tribunal_.281945.E2.80.931946.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuremberg_Tribunal_(1945–1946)">Nuremberg Tribunal (1945–1946)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock,_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2404" data-file-width="2951" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg/200px-Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg/300px-Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg/400px-Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock%2C_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuremberg_Trials._Looking_down_on_defendants_dock,_circa_1945-1946._-_NARA_-_540127.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nazi leaders at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Justice,_Nuremberg" title="Palace of Justice, Nuremberg">Palace of Justice, Nuremberg</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Nazi leaders who were prosecuted shortly after World War II for taking part in the Holocaust, and other mass murders, were charged under existing <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international laws</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Crimes_against_humanity" title="Crimes against humanity">crimes against humanity</a>, as the crime of "genocide' was not formally defined until the 1948 <i>Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide</i> (CPPCG). Nevertheless, the recently coined term<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials#Trial" title="Nuremberg trials">indictment of the Nazi leaders</a>, Count 3, which stated that those charged had "conducted deliberate and systematic genocide—namely, the extermination of racial and national groups—against the civilian populations of certain occupied territories in order to destroy particular races and classes of people, and national, racial or religious groups, particularly Jews, Poles, Gypsies and others."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_Former_Yugoslavia_.281993.E2.80.932017.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_Former_Yugoslavia_(1993–2017)">International Criminal Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia (1993–2017)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_genocide" title="Bosnian genocide">Bosnian genocide</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Bosnian_genocide_prosecutions" title="List of Bosnian genocide prosecutions">List of Bosnian genocide prosecutions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1338" data-file-width="2254" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg/200px-Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg/300px-Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg/400px-Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Srebrenica_massacre_memorial_gravestones_2009_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cemetery at the <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica_Genocide_Memorial" title="Srebrenica Genocide Memorial">Srebrenica-Potočari Memorial and Cemetery to Genocide Victims</a></div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Bosnian genocide</i> is used to refer either to <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica_massacre" title="Srebrenica massacre">the killings</a> committed by Serb forces in <a href="/wiki/Srebrenica" title="Srebrenica">Srebrenica</a> in 1995,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> or to ethnic cleansing that took place elsewhere during the 1992–1995 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="War in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnian War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_Former_Yugoslavia" title="International Criminal Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia">International Criminal Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia</a> (ICTY) judged that the 1995 Srebrenica massacre was an act of genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> On 26 February 2007, the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> (ICJ), in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosnian_Genocide_Case" title="Bosnian Genocide Case">Bosnian Genocide Case</a></i> upheld the ICTY's earlier finding that the massacre in Srebrenica and Zepa constituted genocide, but found that the Serbian government had not participated in a wider genocide on the territory of Bosnia and Herzegovina during the war, as the Bosnian government had claimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 July 2007, <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> when dismissing the appeal by <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Jorgi%C4%87" title="Nikola Jorgić">Nikola Jorgić</a> against his conviction for genocide by a German court (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jorgic_v._Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jorgic v. Germany (page does not exist)">Jorgic v. Germany</a></i>) noted that the German courts wider interpretation of genocide has since been rejected by international courts considering similar cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The ECHR also noted that in the 21st century "Amongst scholars, the majority have taken the view that <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a>, in the way in which it was carried out by the Serb forces in Bosnia and Herzegovina in order to expel Muslims and Croats from their homes, did not constitute genocide. However, there are also a considerable number of scholars who have suggested that these acts did amount to genocide, and the ICTY has found in the Momcilo Krajisnik case that the actus reus of genocide was met in Prijedor "With regard to the charge of genocide, the Chamber found that in spite of evidence of acts perpetrated in the municipalities which constituted the actus reus of genocide".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 30 people have been indicted for participating in genocide or complicity in genocide during the early 1990s in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia</a>. To date, after several <a href="/wiki/Plea_bargain" title="Plea bargain">plea bargains</a> and some convictions that were successfully challenged on appeal two men, <a href="/wiki/Vujadin_Popovi%C4%87" title="Vujadin Popović">Vujadin Popović</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ljubi%C5%A1a_Beara" title="Ljubiša Beara">Ljubiša Beara</a>, have been found guilty of committing genocide, <a href="/wiki/Zdravko_Tolimir" title="Zdravko Tolimir">Zdravko Tolimir</a> has been found guilty of committing genocide and conspiracy to commit genocide, and two others, <a href="/wiki/Radislav_Krsti%C4%87" title="Radislav Krstić">Radislav Krstić</a> and Drago Nikolić, have been found guilty of aiding and abetting genocide. Three others have been found guilty of participating in genocides in Bosnia by German courts, one of whom Nikola Jorgić lost an appeal against his conviction in the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a>. A further eight men, former members of the Bosnian Serb security forces were found guilty of genocide by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Court_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="State Court of Bosnia and Herzegovina">State Court of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_Bosnian_genocide_prosecutions" title="List of Bosnian genocide prosecutions">List of Bosnian genocide prosecutions</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Slobodan Milošević">Slobodan Milošević</a>, as the former President of Serbia and of Yugoslavia, was the most senior political figure to stand trial at the ICTY. He died on 11 March 2006 during his trial where he was accused of genocide or complicity in genocide in territories within Bosnia and Herzegovina, so no verdict was returned. In 1995, the ICTY issued a warrant for the arrest of Bosnian Serbs <a href="/wiki/Radovan_Karad%C5%BEi%C4%87" title="Radovan Karadžić">Radovan Karadžić</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ratko_Mladi%C4%87" title="Ratko Mladić">Ratko Mladić</a> on several charges including genocide. On 21 July 2008, Karadžić was arrested in Belgrade, and later tried in The Hague accused of genocide among other crimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> On 24 March 2016, Karadžić was found guilty of genocide in Srebrenica, war crimes and crimes against humanity, 10 of the 11 charges in total, and sentenced to 40 years' imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt24-03-2016_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt24-03-2016-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Mladić was arrested on 26 May 2011 in Lazarevo, Serbia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and was <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Ratko_Mladi%C4%87" title="Trial of Ratko Mladić">tried in The Hague</a>. The verdict, delivered on 22 November 2017 found Mladić guilty of 10 of the 11 charges, including genocide and he was sentenced to life imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guard_verd_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guard_verd-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda_.281994.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda_(1994–present)">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda (1994–present)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/220px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/330px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg/440px-Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nyamata_Memorial_Site_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victims of the 1994 Rwandan genocide</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_Rwanda" title="International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda">International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda</a> (ICTR) is a court under the auspices of the United Nations for the prosecution of offenses committed in <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">genocide which occurred there</a> during April 1994, commencing on 6 April. The ICTR was created on 8 November 1994 by the Security Council of the United Nations in order to judge those people responsible for the acts of genocide and other serious violations of the international law performed in the territory of Rwanda, or by Rwandan citizens in nearby states, between 1 January and 31 December 1994.
</p><p>So far, the ICTR has finished nineteen trials and convicted twenty-seven accused persons. On 14 December 2009, two more men were accused and convicted for their crimes. Another twenty-five persons are still on trial. Twenty-one are awaiting trial in detention, two more added on 14 December 2009. Ten are still at large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The first trial, of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Akayesu" title="Jean Akayesu">Jean-Paul Akayesu</a>, began in 1997. Akayesu was the first person ever to be convicted of the crime of genocide. In October 1998, Akayesu was sentenced to life imprisonment. <a href="/wiki/Jean_Kambanda" title="Jean Kambanda">Jean Kambanda</a>, interim Prime Minister, pleaded guilty.
</p>
<h4><span id="Extraordinary_Chambers_in_the_Courts_of_Cambodia_.282003.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraordinary_Chambers_in_the_Courts_of_Cambodia_(2003–present)">Extraordinary Chambers in the Courts of Cambodia (2003–present)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Killing_Fields" title="Killing Fields">Killing Fields</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge_Tribunal" title="Khmer Rouge Tribunal">Khmer Rouge Tribunal</a></div>
